          Genes function together within context-specific networks to carry out

          You are a scientist with copy-editing skills tasked with refining the

          repurposing, clustering of complex traits.' Your goal is to revise the
          following paragraph from the Introduction section to enhance clarity,
          reduce jargon, and maintain a scholarly tone. The revision should
          adhere to Markdown formatting and follow a Context-Content-Conclusion
          (C-C-C) structure. This structure begins by setting the stage with one
          or two sentences (Context), progresses through what is known from the
          literature (Content), and concludes by highlighting an aspect of the
          knowledge gap the manuscript addresses (Conclusion). Your revision
          should: 1) preserve the original information as much as possible, with
          minimal changes to the text, 2) ensure most references to scientific
          articles are kept exactly as they appear in the original text; these
          references are crucial for academic integrity and may appear with the
          format "[@TYPE:ID]" such as "[@pmid:33931583;
          @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225; @pmid:31036433]", and 4) the revised
          paragraph must encapsulate the entire revision, following the C-C-C
          structure, within a single, cohesive paragraph.


          Input paragraph: Genes work together in context-specific networks to
          karry out different functions [@pmid:19104045; @doi:10.1038/ng.3259].
          Variations in these genes can change their functional role and, at a
          higher level, affect disease-relevant biological processes
          [@doi:10.1038/s41467-018-06022-6]. In this context, determining how

          expression across different tissues [@doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776].

          @doi:10.1038/s41588-018-0081-4; @doi:10.1038/ncomms6890] has improoved
          the identification of these transcriptional mechanisms that, when
          dysregulated, commonly result in tissue- and cell lineage-specific
          pathology [@pmid:20624743; @pmid:14707169;
          @doi:10.1073/pnas.0810772105].

          The intricate mechanisms of gene regulation play a crucial role in
          shaping complex traits and, ultimately, influencing disease-related
          biological processes. In this context, understanding how genes

          mechanisms, which, when dysregulated, commonly result in tissue- and

          @doi:10.1073/pnas.08.10772105]. Notably, the knowledge gap remains in
          fully elucidating how genes work together in context-specific networks
          to carry out different functions [@pmid:19104045;
          @doi:10.1038/ng.3259], highlighting the need for further research into
          gene regulation and its implications for disease treatment.

          The rapid advancement of genomic research has led to an unprecedented
          availability of gene expression data across multiple tissues
          [@doi:10.1038/nbt.3838; @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6;

          [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618]. However, a key limitation of TWAS lies in its
          focus on individual genes, which neglects the complex interactions
          that occur at the network level, highlighting the need for novel
          approaches to capture these intricate relationships.

          in gene regulatory mechanisms mediate these associations have been
          highly successful in elucidating disease etiology at the transcriptome
          level [@pmid:33931583; @doi:10.1101/2021.10.21.21265225;
          @pmid:31036433] and, more broadly, in disease-risk prediction
          (polygenic scores) [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w] and drug
          repurposing [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618]. The availability of gene
          expression data across several tissues [@doi:10.1038/nbt.3838;
          @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6; @doi:10.1126/science.aaz1776;
          @doi:10.1186/s13040-020-00216-9] has created an opportunity for the
          transcription-wide association study (TWAS) to integrate expression
          quantitative trait loci (eQTLs) data and provide a mechanistic
          interpretation for GWAS findings. However, despite its successes, TWAS
          is limited by its focus on individual genes, failing to capture more
          complex interactions at the network level, thereby highlighting an
          aspect of the knowledge gap that this manuscript aims to address.

          The pursuit of understanding the etiology of human diseases has led to
          the development of various approaches that investigate the
          relationships between genetic variants and gene regulatory mechanisms.
          Notably, the transcription-wide association study (TWAS) has been
          instrumental in elucidating these connections by testing whether

          the limitations of TWAS, which operates at the individual gene level,
          underscore the need for approaches that can capture more complex
          interactions at the network level, thereby bridging a critical
          knowledge gap in our understanding of disease mechanisms.

          The genetic basis of human diseases remains a pressing concern in

          mechanisms underlying disease susceptibility. In this context,

          The availability of gene expression data across several tissues
          [@doi:10.1038/nbt.3838; @doi:10.1038/s41467-018-03751-6;

          and, more broadly, in disease-risk prediction (polygenic scores)
          [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w] and drug repurposing
          [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618]. However, there remains a pressing need to
          develop approaches that can elucidate complex interactions at the
          network level, thereby illuminating disease etiology and therapeutic
          targets.

              Genes function together within context-specific networks to carry
              out diverse functions [1, 2]. Variations in these genes can alter

              disease-relevant biological processes [3]. To understand how genes
              influence complex traits, it is essential to mechanistically grasp

              should ultimately lead to improved treatments [7, 8]. Previous
              studies have described various regulatory DNA elements [9-13],

              genome-wide association study (GWAS) data [15-18] has enhanced the
              identification of these transcriptional mechanisms that, when

              variants and human diseases [@doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1009482;
              @doi:10.1038/ng.3506; @doi:10.1371/journal.pgen.1007889;

              and predict disease risk (polygenic scores)
              [@doi:10.1186/s13059-021-02591-w], as well as facilitate drug
              repurposing tasks [@doi:10.1038/nn.4618]. However, a key
              limitation of TWAS is its focus on individual genes, which
              neglects more complex interactions at the network level,
              highlighting the need for approaches that can capture these
              intricate relationships.
